Specification and Generalization Explained
Maturidi explains why volition is general and love/pleasure are specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why volition is general and love/pleasure are specific, and the role of power in volition.

Core Idea
God's volition is universal because it is linked to His absolute power.

After establishing the Quranic evidence, Maturidi explains why this distinction holds. He notes that volition in God is directed to His power, making it comprehensive, whereas love and pleasure are not attributes of power but of approval.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the short Arabic passage. It states that volition is directed to power and thus under the rule of compulsion (qasr), and that this is why its power necessitates the effect.
The phrase 'biḥukm al-qasr' means under the rule of compulsion, indicating necessity.
2
Understand 'specification'
Love and pleasure are only in some actions, not all. They are 'specified' because they are not used for every action.
3
Understand 'generalization'
Will and volition are 'general' because they apply to all actions that occur. This is because volition is tied to God's power, which encompasses everything.
This explains why volition is universal.

The Foundation and Human Example
Maturidi explains the foundation and gives a human example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational principle that love/anger are caused by servants' actions while will accompanies the action itself, supported by a human example.

Core Idea
Love follows action; will accompanies action.

Maturidi now explains the deeper metaphysical basis: love and anger are meanings that arise from the actions of servants, whereas will is not dependent on the action. He then provides a human example to illustrate that one can do what one does not love but cannot do what one does not will.

Guided Path
1
Read the foundation statement
The passage states: 'Love and anger are two meanings necessitated by the action of servants.' Understand that they are responses to actions.
2
Contrast with volition
Volition is not like that; there is nothing in the servants' actions that necessitates volition. Volition is inherent in the act itself.
The phrase 'ma'na yu- al-mashi'a' likely means 'that necessitates volition'.
3
Study the human example
In the witnessed world (shahid), a person may do something he is not pleased with and does not love, but he cannot do a real action that he does not will. This proves will is inseparable from action, while love is separable.
This is an analogy from the created to the Creator (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid).
4
Distinguish Maturidi's view from others
Maturidi notes that others (the Mu'tazila?) consider will prior to action, while he says it accompanies the action and has no aspect after it. Love and pleasure, by contrast, occur after the action in common usage.

Objection on God's Desire for Ease and the Answer
Maturidi responds to the objection that God wills ease yet disbelief is hardship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that God's desire for ease contradicts His allowing disbelief, by distinguishing between volitional will and permissive will.

Core Idea
God's desire for ease means permission, not that He wills faith for all.

This passage presents an opponent's argument based on the Quranic verse 'God desires ease for you' to claim that God must desire faith for everyone. Maturidi responds by clarifying that divine 'will' (irādah) in such contexts means permission, allowance, or concession, not God's effective will that determines human actions. This distinction is crucial for understanding how divine attributes relate to human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow: the opponent's citation, then the two-part answer.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
Note the logic: God says He desires ease, not hardship; disbelief is the ultimate hardship; therefore God must not desire disbelief. Recognize why this seems problematic.
3
Understand Maturidi's first response
He reinterprets 'desire' (irādah) in the verse as permission (idhn) and license (rukhsah), not volitional will. This removes the contradiction.
4
Understand the second response
The verse addresses believers who already chose faith; if God willed faith for disbelievers, they would become believers. The objection fails.
This uses verses showing God wills the Hereafter for believers but not for disbelievers.
5
Reflect on the key distinction
Distinguish between God's legislative will (permission) and His existential will (determination). This is central to Maturidi's theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم / احتج بقوله تعالى : يريد الله بكم اليسر ، ونحو ذلك، وقال: ولا يريد بكم العسر ، والكفر أعسر العسر. قيل : الإرادة في هذا تخرج على الإذن والإباحة والرخصة، ليس ذلك من أمر الإيمان في شيء، فكذا [عدم] إرادة العسر. وأيضا إنه لو كان على الأمرين فالوجه [ أن ] أولئك قوم قد آمنوا فلم يكن لهم في التحقيق غير الذي أراد، فلو كان من الكافر أراد الإيمان لكان لا يكون سواه، كما إذا أراد فيمن أراد الإيمان لم يكن غيره. وعلى هذا قوله تعالى فمن يرد الله أن يهديه ، الآية " ، وأيّد ذلك قوله : يريد الله ألا يجعل لهم حظا في الآخرة ؛ وبيّن في المؤمنين : والله يريد الآخرة ، دل أن كل من أراد له الإيمان [ ينبغي] أن يكون فعله أراد له الآخرة، ومن لم يرد لا. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he (the opponent) argued with His saying: "God desires ease for you" and similar verses, and said: "And He does not desire hardship for you," and disbelief is the hardest of hardship. It was said: The will (al-irādah) in this is interpreted as permission (al-idhn), allowance (al-ibāḥah), and license (al-rukhṣah); that has nothing to do with the matter of faith. So likewise the non-desire of hardship. And also, if it (the will) were for both matters, then the aspect is that those people had already believed, so in reality there was nothing for them other than what He willed. So if the disbeliever had willed faith, he would not be other than it, just as when He wills for someone faith, he is not other than it. And on this is His saying: "Whoever God wills to guide..." and He supported that with His saying: "God wills not to assign them a portion in the Hereafter" and He made clear for the believers: "And God desires the Hereafter," indicating that whoever He wills for him faith, his action should be that He willed for him the Hereafter, and whoever He does not will, not. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from an opponent who cites the verse 'God desires ease for you' and argues that disbelief is hardship, so God must not desire disbelief. The answer is that 'desire' (irādah) here means permission or allowance, not volitional will. Furthermore, the context of the verse addresses believers who have already chosen faith, so their state aligns with what God wills. The opponent's reading would imply that if God willed faith for a disbeliever, he would believe, but that is not the case. Maturidi reinforces this with verses that show God's will for believers is the Hereafter, while He wills no share for disbelievers. Thus, the objection is refuted by distinguishing between types of divine will.

إذن
إباحة

يسر
عسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'God desires ease for you' means God volitionally wills ease for all people, so He must will faith for everyone and not allow disbelief.
Correction: Maturidi explains that 'desire' here refers to permissive will (idhn, ibāḥah, rukhṣah), not God's effective, determinative will.

Checkpoint

A) It means God wills ease universally.
B) It means God's desire is unconditional.
C) It means permission and allowance, not volitional will.
D) It contradicts divine justice.

Key Takeaway
God's desire for ease in the Quran denotes permissive will, not volitional determinism, preserving human accountability.

Answering the Objection: God Does Not Will Injustice
Maturidi responds to the claim that God's will for disbelief entails injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's willing of disbelief does not constitute divine injustice.

Core Idea
God wills the act of disbelief for just recompense, not injustice.

This lesson addresses a critical objection against divine will: if God wills disbelief, does He become unjust? Maturidi refutes this by distinguishing between willing an unjust act and willing injustice to the servant. He uses Quranic analogies to clarify that God's will for a sinful action is part of a just order.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and then the English translation. Try to identify the objection being made and the core of Maturidi's response.
2
Analyze the analogy
Notice how Maturidi uses the Quranic verse about 'bāṭil' (falsehood) as an analogy. Reflect on why calling something 'bāṭil' does not make it false in itself.
This analogy is key to understanding the argument.
3
Apply the reasoning
Consider how the analogy applies to the case of disbelief: God wills the act, but He does not will injustice to the servant. Instead, He wills just recompense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج بقول الله تعالى: ﴿وما الله يريد ظلما للعباد قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } فنقول كذلك، ومن أراد عداوة إنسان [ف]له عداوة [عليه]؛ أو فعله الظلم قبيحا فاحشاً ، فليس بمريد لهم الظلم بل أراد لهم العدل. قال الله تعالى: ﴿وما خلقنا السماء والأرض وما بينهما باطلا ﴾ [ص، ۲۷/۳۸] ، ثم قال في القرآن : لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه ﴾ [السجدة، ٤٢/٤١] ، سماه باطلاً ، لا أن خلقه باطلاً. فمثله إرادة فعل الكفر من الكافر باطلاً وظلما لا يكون منه إرادة الظلم للعباد، وتأويله قوله : وما ربك بظلام للعبيد ﴾ [السجدة، ٤٦/٤١] . وبعد، فإنه في الاعتبار به جائز، لأن إرادة ما يعلم أن يكون ليكون عدل ، إذ هو أراد جزاء فعله لا أن يعاقبه على أمر لم يفعله. والله الكافي.
And he argued with the saying of God Almighty: "And God does not will injustice for the servants." The Shaykh (God have mercy on him) said: So we say likewise, and whoever wills enmity for a person, then he has enmity against him; or His action of injustice is ugly and obscene, so He is not willing injustice for them, but rather He willed justice for them. God Almighty said: "And We did not create the heavens and the earth and what is between them in falsehood (bāṭil)" [38:27], then He said in the Quran: "Falsehood does not approach it from before it or from behind it" [41:42], He called it falsehood, not that His creation is falsehood. Similarly, the will for the action of disbelief from the disbeliever is falsehood and injustice, but He does not thereby will injustice for the servants, and its interpretation is His saying: "And your Lord is not unjust to His servants" [41:46]. Moreover, it is permissible in consideration, because willing what is known to be, in order that it be justice, since He willed the recompense for his action, not that He punishes him for something he did not do. And God is sufficient.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents an objection based on the verse 'God does not will injustice for the servants,' implying that if God willed disbelief from a disbeliever, He would be willing injustice. Maturidi's answer: God does not will injustice, but He wills actions that are themselves unjust (from the servant) in order to justly requite them. He uses an analogy: God calls the Quran 'falsehood' in one verse and denies falsehood in another—this does not mean the Quran is false, but rather He calls something falsehood without it being so from Him. Likewise, God's willing the act of disbelief (which is unjust from the servant) does not mean He wills injustice for the servant; rather, He wills justice by rewarding or punishing accordingly. The act is a means to a just end. Thus, the objection is refuted: divine will for a sinful act does not entail divine injustice.

Key Terms
ظلم
عدل
باطل
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills a sinful act, He becomes unjust.
Correction: Maturidi shows that willing an act for just recompense is not injustice.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi refute the claim that if God wills disbelief, He would be willing injustice?
A) He denies that God wills disbelief.
B) He argues that God wills disbelief only for the Hereafter.
C) He says God wills the act but not the injustice; He wills just recompense.
D) He says the verse refers only to believers.

Key Takeaway
God's will for disbelief is part of a just system of reward and punishment, not an act of injustice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own sins, do you ever feel that God is unjust for allowing them? How does Maturidi's distinction between willing the action and willing recompense change your perspective? Will you now accept His justice even when you don't understand?
?
If God wills disbelief in some people, does that make you feel secure in your own faith? Or does it humble you? What does this lesson teach you about your own responsibility to choose belief?
?
Consider a time you felt God was unjust toward you. Based on this lesson, how can you trust that His will is always just, even when it involves hardship or the evil actions of others? Will you commit to trusting His justice today, even if you cannot see the full picture?

The Prophet's Will for Defeat: Wilful Act vs Sinful Intent
Maturidi answers the objection that the Prophet willed the polytheists' defeat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between willing an act that is sinful and willing the sin itself, and how this resolves objections about divine and prophetic will.

Core Idea
Willing an act is not willing the sin; intent matters.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: if the Prophet willed the defeat of the polytheists (an act of disobedience), doesn't that imply approval of sin? Maturidi carefully distinguishes between willing an act that happens to be sinful and willing the sin itself. He uses Quranic examples and analogies from believers' sins to clarify this distinction, and then addresses a related objection about the Prophet's pleasure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection, answer, example, analogy, application, second objection.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the core distinction
Maturidi argues that willing an act that is sinful is not the same as willing the sin itself. Find how he supports this.
The key is the difference between the act's nature and the intention behind it.
3
Analyze the Quranic example
The verse 'I intend that you bear my sin and your sin' is used as evidence. Understand how this illustrates the distinction.
The brother willed the killing, but not the sin of murder as such.
4
Apply the analogy to believers and God
Maturidi extends the reasoning: believers' sinful actions are not intended as sin, and similarly God's will for disbelief is not willing sin itself.
This defends divine justice.
5
Examine the secondary objection
The objection that the Prophet was 'pleased' with defeat is dismissed because the defeat was not caused by the Prophet or God.
Pleasure or displeasure applies only to one's own actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سئل عن إرادة رسول الله انهزام المشركين ، فزعم أنه أراد لينظروا فيما دعاهم إليه . قال الفقيه رحمه الله : } فالانهزام طاعة أو معصية، وكذلك الحال إلى وقت النظر وفي ذلك دوام على المعصية ؟ لا بد أن يقول : معصية . فيجوز أن يراد به ـ لا على قصدها ـ لبعض المصالح. ومثله قوله : إني أريد أن تبوء / بإثمي وإثمك ؛ إنه يجوز إرادة فعل هو معصية لا على قصد عصيان وكذلك معاصي المؤمنين كلها كانت على أفعال من عاصيهم، وإن لم يريدوا معصية الله، بل لو أرادوا الكفروا . فهذا يبيّن أن إرادة فعل يكون من فاعله معصية لا يكون كإرادة المعصية. فمثله إرادة الله فعل الكافر ليكون منه معصية، أو فعله الشتم ليكون شتما " " قبيحاً لا يكون كإرادة الشتم والمعصية. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم عارض بأن رسول الله رضي منهم الانهزام وذلك فاسد، لأن الانهزام منهم لم يكن الرسول الله أو الله فيتكلم فيه بالرضا وغير الرضا . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he asked about the will of the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) for the defeat of the polytheists, and he claimed that he willed so that they would consider what he called them to. The jurist (God have mercy on him) said: Is defeat an act of obedience or disobedience? And likewise the situation up to the time of consideration, and is there persistence in disobedience in that? He must say: disobedience. So it is permissible to will it—not intending it as sin—for some benefits. And similar to this is his saying: "I intend that you bear my sin and your sin" [5:29]; indeed, it is permissible to will an action that is an act of disobedience without intending disobedience. Similarly, all the disobedient acts of the believers were according to actions of those who disobeyed Him, even though they did not intend disobedience to God; rather, if they had intended it, they would have disbelieved. This clarifies that willing an action that is from its doer an act of disobedience is not like willing disobedience. So similarly, God's willing the action of the disbeliever so that from him comes disobedience, or His action of reviling so that it becomes ugly reviling, is not like willing reviling and disobedience. And there is no power except through God. Then he objected that the Messenger of God was pleased with their defeat, and that is invalid, because their defeat was not from the Messenger of God or God, to speak of pleasure or displeasure regarding it. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection: the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) willed the defeat of the polytheists so they would consider Islam. The questioner implies that if the Prophet's will for defeat (a possible sin) is permissible, then God's will for disbelief would also be permissible. The answer: defeat here is an act of disobedience (sin) from the polytheists, but the Prophet did not will it as sin; rather, he willed it for a good end (consideration). This is like the verse of Cain's brother who said 'I intend that you bear my sin and your sin'—he willed the act (killing) not as sin but as a means. Similarly, believers' sins are actions they perform without intending sin. Therefore, willing an act that is sinful in itself is not the same as willing the sin. So God's willing the disbeliever's act of disbelief is not willing the sin as sin, but willing the act for just punishment. A secondary objection about the Prophet's pleasure is dismissed because the defeat was not from the Prophet's action.

معصية
انهزام
قصد
رضا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Willing a sinful act implies approval of the sin itself.
Correction: Maturidi shows that one can will an act that is sinful without intending the sin, as when the Prophet willed defeat for a higher purpose or the brother willed killing without intending sin.

Checkpoint
What is the core reasoning Maturidi uses to show that God's will for disbelief is not willing sin?
A) God does not actually will disbelief.
B) Willing an act that is sinful is different from willing the sin itself when done for a just purpose.
C) Disbelief is not truly a sin.
D) The Prophet's example proves all will is good.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's will for defeat was for a higher purpose, not as willing sin; similarly, God's will for disbelief is not willing the sin itself but the act for just punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever tell yourself that you didn't 'really' intend the disobedience—yet in your heart, you knew it was wrong? How does this lesson force you to honestly examine the intention behind your actions?
?
The Prophet willed a sinful act for a higher purpose, not as sin itself. Have you ever justified a questionable action by claiming your intention was good, while deep down you knew it was still a sin? What would Maturidi's distinction reveal about your self-deception?
?
If God wills the disbeliever's act for punishment, not as willing sin itself, what does that say about your own accountability? Are you willing to accept that your sins are entirely your own doing, even though God wills the existence of the act? How must this change your plea for forgiveness?

God Does Not Will Injustice
The author proves that God does not will injustice using Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author demonstrates that God does not will injustice for His servants, specifically by distinguishing between willing an act and willing its injustice.

Core Idea
God wills the act but not the injustice.

In this passage, the author defends God's justice by clarifying what it means when we say 'God does not will injustice.' He uses Quranic evidence to show that willing an act of disbelief is not the same as willing injustice. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's flow of argument come through.
Notice the key verse: 'And God does not will injustice for the servants.'
2
Identify the central distinction
The author distinguishes between willing an act (e.g., disbelief) and willing the injustice inherent in that act. Find where he makes this distinction.
Look for the analogy about willing the act of disbelief.
3
Examine the Quranic proofs
The author cites three verses to support his claim. Identify each one and see how it reinforces his argument.
Verses: 40:31, 38:27, 41:42, and 41:46.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with a theological consideration: God wills recompense for actions, not punishment for what was not done. See how this ties back to justice.
This shows God's will is always just.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج بقول الله تعالى: ﴿وما الله يريد ظلما للعباد﴾ قال الشيخ رحمه الله : فنقول كذلك، ومن أراد عداوة إنسان فله عداوة عليه؛ أو فعله الظلم قبيحاً فاحشاً، فليس بمريد لهم الظلم بل أراد لهم العدل. قال الله تعالى: ﴿وما خلقنا السماء والأرض وما بينهما باطلاً﴾ [ص: 38:27]، ثم قال في القرآن: ﴿لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه﴾ [السجدة: 41:42]، سماه باطلاً، لا أن خلقه باطلاً. فمثله إرادة فعل الكفر من الكافر باطلاً وظلماً لا يكون منه إرادة الظلم للعباد، وتأويله قوله: ﴿وما ربك بظلام للعبيد﴾ [السجدة: 41:46]. وبعد، فإنه في الاعتبار به جائز، لأن إرادة ما يعلم أن يكون ليكون عدل، إذ هو أراد جزاء فعله لا أن يعاقبه على أمر لم يفعله. والله الكافي.
He argued by the saying of God Almighty: 'And God does not will injustice for the servants.' The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: So we say likewise. And whoever wills enmity for a person, he has enmity against him; or his action of injustice is ugly and foul, so he is not willing injustice for them but rather willed justice for them. God Almighty said: 'And We did not create the heaven and the earth and what is between them in vain.' Then He said in the Quran: 'Falsehood does not come to it from before it or from behind it.' He called it falsehood, not that His creation is falsehood. Similarly, willing the act of disbelief from the disbeliever, false and unjust, does not constitute willing injustice for the servants. Its interpretation is His saying: 'And your Lord is not unjust to the servants.' Moreover, it is permissible in consideration, because willing what is known to be is to be justice, since He willed the recompense of his action, not that He punishes him for something he did not do. And God is Sufficient.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God does not will injustice for His servants. It begins by citing the Quranic verse 'And God does not will injustice for the servants' (Ghafir 40:31). The Shaykh affirms this and explains: if someone wills enmity against another, that enmity is upon the one who wills it, and if an act of injustice is ugly and foul, then the agent is not willing injustice for them but rather wills justice. He supports this with the verses 'We did not create the heaven and the earth... in vain' (Sad 38:27) and 'Falsehood does not come to it' (Fussilat 41:42), distinguishing between something being false in itself and God's act of creating not being false. Similarly, willing the act of disbelief from the disbeliever—though false and unjust—does not constitute willing injustice for the servants, as interpreted by 'Your Lord is not unjust to the servants' (Fussilat 41:46). Finally, he notes that God's will for what He knows will happen is just, because He wills the just recompense of the servant's own action, not punishment for something not done.

ظلم

باطل

Correction: The passage clarifies that willing the act of disbelief is not the same as willing injustice; God wills the act but not its inherent injustice.

Checkpoint

B) God does not will any act of the servants.
C) God wills only justice, and any act that appears unjust is not actually willed by Him.
D) God wills injustice for disbelievers but not for believers.

Key Takeaway
God's will for acts of disbelief does not imply that He wills injustice for His servants; He wills the act but not its injustice.

Distinguishing God's Will from His Pleasure
Maturidi refutes the objection about Iblis's will by distinguishing will from pleasure/love.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that Iblis's will overcomes God's will is invalid by distinguishing between God's will (which is a condition for action) and His pleasure/love/anger (which are responses to action).

Core Idea
God's will is a condition for action; His pleasure is not.

A common objection against predestination is that Iblis's will to mislead seems to override God's will for obedience. Maturidi responds by clarifying that God's will is not the same as His pleasure or love. This passage explains that distinction and shows that God does not love or is pleased with those He knows will not believe.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Identify the objection ('ثم عورض') and the answer using pleasure, love, anger.
The objection is that Iblis's will prevails over God's.
2
Understand the distinction
Notice that will (إرادة) is a condition for action, while pleasure (رضا) and love (محبة) can occur without the action happening at that moment. This means God can will something without being pleased with it.
3
Apply to God's love
The passage says we do not claim that God loves or is pleased with someone He knows will not disbelieve, because love and pleasure are based on action. So God can will disbelief without loving it.
This refutes the objection that God's will must be what He is pleased with.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عورض بما كان كفر أكثر عباد الله بأن أراد إبليس، والله أراد منهم الطاعة، فصارت إرادة إبليس في ملك الله وسلطانه أبعد من إرادة الله. فأجاب بالرضا والمحبة والسخط، وقد بيّنا نحن الفصل بين الأمرين على أنه يكون فعل يرضى به المرء ويسخط من غير أن كان وقت فعله، ومحال ذلك في الإرادة؛ ثبت أنها شرط الفعل فيما يُظهر التعجيز إذ لا يخلو عنها عنه، لأنهما فعل المختار وأيضا إنا لا نقول بأن الله يحب من يعلم أنه لا يؤمن أو يرضى عنه، يجبان بالفعل، فمن لا يفعل فالقول به بعيد؛ وأما الإرادة فقد بيّنا. والله أعلم. والأصل في هذا، في المتعارف أن الفعل يخرج على إرادة أو غلبة أو غفلة. ثم الله سبحانه لا يجوز أن يوصف في فعل العبد بالغلبة أو بالغفلة، ثبت أن كان بالإرادة والمعتزلة لا يثبتون الله معنى في الإرادة سوى كون العلم بعد أن لم يكن من غير ضرورة له، وهذا المعنى هو في فعل كل من أهل العالم قائم، فلا وجه لإنكارهم على قولهم. وبالله العصمة.
Then he was objected with the fact that most of God's servants disbelieved because Iblis willed, and God willed from them obedience, so the will of Iblis in God's kingdom and authority is more far-reaching than God's will. So he answered with pleasure, love, and anger. And we have explained the distinction between the two matters: that there is an action that one is pleased with and angered by without it being at the time of its occurrence, and that is impossible regarding will; it is established that will is a condition of the action in what shows incapability, since it is not free from it, because they are the action of the free chooser. Also, we do not say that God loves someone He knows will not believe or is pleased with him; these two (love and pleasure) are by action, so the one who does not act, saying so is far-fetched. As for will, we have explained. And God knows best. The principle in this, in common usage, is that action occurs either by will, or by compulsion, or by forgetfulness. Then God Almighty cannot be described in the action of the servant by compulsion or forgetfulness; it is established that it is by will. And the Mu'tazila do not affirm for God any meaning in will other than knowledge after non-existence without necessity, and this meaning exists in the action of everyone in the world, so there is no reason for their denial of our statement. And with God is protection.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its answer. The objection states that since most disbelieve, Iblis's will seems more effective than God's will for obedience. The answer distinguishes between will (irada) and pleasure/love/anger (rida, mahabbah, sukht). One can be pleased or angered by an action without it occurring at that moment, but will is always a condition for the action of a free agent. God does not love or is pleased with someone He knows will not believe, because love and pleasure are based on action. Will, however, is a necessary condition. The passage then states a principle: actions occur by will, compulsion, or forgetfulness. God cannot be described by compulsion or forgetfulness regarding human acts, so they must occur by His will. The Mu'tazila reduce God's will to mere knowledge of what will happen, but this meaning applies to all agents, so their denial of a distinct will is unfounded.

Key Terms
إرادة
رضا
محبة
سخط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's will and His pleasure are identical, so whatever God wills must be what He loves.
Correction: The passage shows that will is a condition for action, while pleasure is a response to action. God can will something He is not pleased with, such as disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is will different from pleasure?
A) Will is a condition for action; pleasure can occur without action.
B) Will is eternal, pleasure is temporal.
C) Will is for all things, pleasure only for good.
D) Will is known, pleasure is unknown.

Key Takeaway
God's will is a necessary condition for action, while His pleasure is a response to action; they are not identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If God's will is a condition for every action, but His pleasure is not, are you content to merely exist within His will, or do you actively seek actions that bring His love? What one change can you make today to pursue His pleasure rather than simply following what He wills?

Actions Occur by Will, Not Compulsion or Forgetfulness
Maturidi states the principle of action categories and critiques the Mu'tazila view of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that actions occur by will, compulsion, or forgetfulness, and why God cannot be described by compulsion or forgetfulness, thus affirming divine will in human actions. You will also learn the Mu'tazila reduction of will to knowledge and why it is inadequate.

Core Idea
Actions occur by will, compulsion, or forgetfulness; God is free from compulsion and forgetfulness, so human actions occur by His will.

Maturidi establishes a fundamental principle: every action occurs either by will, compulsion, or forgetfulness. Since God is beyond compulsion and forgetfulness, human actions must be by His will. He then critiques the Mu'tazila who reduce God's will to mere knowledge of what will happen, showing that this meaning is universal and does not justify their denial of a distinct divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'والأصل في هذا' and its translation. Note the three categories: will, compulsion, forgetfulness.
This is a general principle in common usage.
2
Apply to God
Understand that God cannot be described by compulsion or forgetfulness regarding human actions, so it must be by will.
3
Critique the Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila do not attribute a distinct meaning to God's will beyond knowledge. Maturidi argues that this meaning (knowledge) exists in all agents, so their denial of a separate will is unfounded.
This supports the Ash'ari/Maturidi view of will as a distinct attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then he was objected with the fact that most of God's servants disbelieved because Iblis willed, and God willed from them obedience, so the will of Iblis in God's kingdom and authority is more far-reaching than God's will. So he answered with pleasure, love, and anger. And we have explained the distinction between the two matters: that there is an action that one is pleased with and angered by without it being at the time of its occurrence, and that is impossible regarding will; it is established that will is a condition of the action in what shows incapability, since it is not free from it, because they are the action of the free chooser. Also, we do not say that God loves someone He knows will not believe or is pleased with him; these two (love and pleasure) are by action, so the one who does not act, saying so is far-fetched. As for will, we have explained. And God knows best. The principle in this, in common usage, is that action occurs either by will, or by compulsion, or by forgetfulness. Then God Almighty cannot be described in the action of the servant by compulsion or forgetfulness; it is established that it is by will. And the Mu'tazila do not affirm for God any meaning in will other than knowledge after non-existence without necessity, and this meaning exists in the action of everyone in the world, so there is no reason for their denial of our statement. And with God is protection.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its answer. The objection states that since most disbelieve, Iblis's will seems more effective than God's will for obedience. The answer distinguishes between will (irada) and pleasure/love/anger (rida, mahabbah, sukht). One can be pleased or angered by an action without it occurring at that moment, but will is always a condition for the action of a free agent. God does not love or is pleased with someone He knows will not believe, because love and pleasure are based on action. Will, however, is a necessary condition. The passage then states a principle: actions occur by will, compulsion, or forgetfulness. God cannot be described by compulsion or forgetfulness regarding human acts, so they must occur by His will. The Mu'tazila reduce God's will to mere knowledge of what will happen, but this meaning applies to all agents, so their denial of a distinct will is unfounded.

Key Terms
إرادة
غلبة
غفلة
معتزلة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will is just His knowledge of what will happen, and that no separate attribute of will is needed.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if will is only knowledge, then that meaning exists in all agents, so it doesn't justify denying a distinct will in God. The principle of action categories shows will is distinct from compulsion and forgetfulness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must human actions be by God's will?
A) Because actions occur by will, compulsion, or forgetfulness, and God cannot be described by compulsion or forgetfulness.
B) Because the Mu'tazila say so.
C) Because will is the same as knowledge.
D) Because Iblis's will is stronger.

Key Takeaway
Human actions are by God's will, and the Mu'tazila reduction of will to knowledge is flawed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever attributed a hardship or failure in your life to ‘just happening’ or ‘bad luck’ rather than seeing it as occurring by Allah’s will? This lesson says every action is by will, not compulsion or forgetfulness. What would it mean for you to sincerely say, ‘This happened by Allah’s will’ about a recent trial? Will you say that right now?
?
The Mu’tazila reduced God’s will to mere knowledge so they could avoid attributing evil to Him. Are you sometimes more concerned with defending your own intellectual comfort (how can a good God allow this?) than with submitting to His absolute will? Identify one area of your life where you have resisted God’s will because it didn’t fit your understanding, and commit tonight to say: ‘I accept this by His will, even if I don’t understand.’
?
If you truly believed that every single action you take—including your repentance and your sins—occurs only by God’s will, how would that change the intensity of your gratitude for good deeds and your remorse for mistakes? Would you still blame yourself as if you are independent? This week, when you catch yourself feeling proud of a good deed, pause and attribute it entirely to Allah’s will. How does that feel?

Iblis's Will as Wish: A Refutation
Refutation of Iblis's will as wish and rejection of divine force.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that Iblis's will is mere wishful thinking is incoherent given the difference between will and wish.

Core Idea
Wish cannot overcome will; Iblis's rebellion cannot be mere wish.

In this passage, the author presents a critic's claim that Iblis's will was simply wishful thinking and that God could have prevented disbelief. He then refutes this by distinguishing will from wish and rejecting the notion of divine force as confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and refutation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the Faqih responds line by line.
The passage starts with the opponent's claim, then the Faqih's rebuttal.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the Faqih distinguishes between will (irada) and wishful thinking (tamanni). Understand why this distinction is crucial.
Will can lead to compulsion; wish cannot.
3
Understand the rhetorical question
The Faqih asks how Iblis's wish could overcome God's will. This exposes the absurdity of the opponent's position.
4
Rejection of divine force
The passage ends by dismissing the idea that God uses force (qahr) as a sign of confusion. Note why this is impermissible.
Belief in compulsion is rejected entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : إرادة إبليس هي التمنى، ولو أراد العباد ما كفروا، والله يقدر على منعهم بالقهر. { قال الفقيه رحمه الله : { قلنا له: قد صدقت؛ والإرادة قد توجب الغلبة، والتمنى لا، فكيف غلب تمنى عدوه على إرادته؟ وقوله : يقدر ويقهر، وهذا النوع إنما هو أثر الحيرة والوحشة، ولا يجوز الإيمان بالقسر بوجه. ثم قال : فإن قيل: هل رأيت حكيما يقدر على منع عبده عن أمر لا يريده ولا يمنعه؟ فعارض بالجبر . وذا خطأ، لأنه عندنا يريده، وليس المنع من شرط ما يراد. ثم قال : فإنه يعد لوجهين لا يريده، ولا يجوز له المنع لضرب من التدبير . قال الشيخ رحمه [ الله ]: إن كنت على الشاهد تُقدره فلا تجده إلا أن لا يقدر عليه أو
Then he said: Iblis's will is wishful thinking; if the servants had willed, they would not have disbelieved, and God is able to prevent them by force. The Faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: We said to him: You have spoken truth; will may necessitate compulsion, but wishful thinking does not. So how did the wishful thinking of His enemy overcome His will? And his saying: 'He is able and forces' — this type is merely the effect of confusion and alienation, and it is not permissible to believe in force in any way. Then he said: If it is said: Have you seen a wise person who is able to prevent his servant from something he does not will and does not prevent him? He objected by (saying it is) compulsion. This is an error, because in our view He wills it, and prevention is not a condition of what is willed. Then he said: Indeed, He counts it for two aspects that He does not will, and it is not permissible for Him to prevent for a certain management. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: If you consider by analogy, you will not find that unless He is unable to prevent or...
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a series of claims and refutations. First, someone claims that Iblis's will is mere wishful thinking (tamanni), and that if the servants had willed, they would not have disbelieved, and God can prevent them by force. The Faqih responds: you are correct that will can necessitate compulsion (ghalaba), but wish cannot; so how could Iblis's wish overcome God's will? He dismisses the idea of God forcing as a result of confusion. Then a second question is posed: if a wise person can prevent his servant from something he does not will, why does God not prevent? The Faqih objects that this implies compulsion (jabr), which is wrong, because God does will the act, and prevention is not a condition of what is willed. Finally, the Shaykh gives an analogical argument, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
تمنى
غلبة
قهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Iblis's will was mere wish and that God could have forced him to obey.
Correction: This passage shows that will necessitates ability to compel, which would make Iblis's rebellion impossible if it were mere wish. Also, belief in divine force is rejected.

Checkpoint
According to the Faqih, what is the key difference between will (irada) and wishful thinking (tamanni)?
A) Will can lead to compulsion, wish cannot.
B) Wish is stronger than will.
C) Will is eternal, wish is temporal.
D) Wish is only for evil.

Key Takeaway
Will and wish are distinct; wish cannot overcome God's will, and divine force is not a valid concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a promise to Allah to change your ways, is your will a firm determination or just a wish that fades? How can you tell the difference, and what does this lesson demand of you?
?
Have you ever attributed your failure to obey Allah to 'His will' as if He forced you to sin? How does this passage expose that excuse as a form of confusion and alienation from true faith?
?
If you truly believe that God does not compel belief, why do you still act as if your sins are beyond your will to abandon? What would it look like to treat your will as real and effective, not a mere wish?

Divine Prevention and the Error of Compulsion
Refutation of the objection that God should prevent disbelief, and clarification on compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's ability to prevent does not imply compulsion, and how willing an act does not require its prevention.

Core Idea
Prevention is not a condition of what is willed.

The discussion moves to a second objection: if a wise person can prevent what he does not will, why does God not prevent disbelief? The Faqih responds that this reasoning mistakenly implies compulsion (jabr), and clarifies that prevention is not a necessary feature of what is willed.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the opponent's question and the Faqih's initial reaction.
The opponent asks about a wise person preventing what he does not will.
2
Understand the accusation of jabr
The Faqih says this objection leads to compulsion (jabr). Note why this is an error.
In our view, God wills the act, but willing does not necessitate prevention.
3
Grasp the management argument
The passage mentions two aspects God does not will and a forbidden prevention due to divine management (tadbir). Reflect on the nuance.
4
Consider the analogical argument
The Shaykh gives an analogy from observable reality (shahid) to the unseen. Though the text cuts off, try to infer the point.
The analogy likely shows that wise beings do not prevent unless they are unable to.

Source Text

Correction: This passage shows that willing does not entail prevention, and the objection leads to a false view of compulsion.

Checkpoint

B) It leads to compulsion (jabr).

?

?
The lesson mentions divine management (tadbir) as a reason for non-prevention. Are you willing to trust that God’s wisdom in allowing trials—even your own failures—is deeper than your desire for a comfortable life? Will you surrender your demand for a problem-free existence and instead seek His purpose in the struggle?

Maturidi's Interpretation of 'If Your Lord Willed'
Maturidi interprets Quran 10:99 as a will of compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's explanation of Quran 10:99 as referring to a will of compulsion rather than guidance, and why this preserves human responsibility.

Core Idea
God's will to compel belief is not the same as His will to guide.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi unpacks a seemingly deterministic verse. He argues that when the Quran says 'If your Lord willed, all would believe,' it refers to a will of compulsion — God could force faith, but He chooses not to. This distinction is crucial for upholding human accountability and the purpose of trials.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and opening statement
Begin by reading Quran 10:99 and Maturidi's initial comment that it is about compulsion.
Notice how he immediately defines the will as compulsory, not guiding.
2
Identify the two types of will
Maturidi distinguishes between a will that compels (force) and a will that tests (trial). Observe how he uses physical attributes as an analogy.
He says: 'just as He made them old and young' — unchangeable.
3
Examine the supporting verse and conclusion
Study his citation of Quran 47:4 and his conclusion that this will of compulsion carries no praise or reward.
This reinforces that the verse does not negate human choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في قوله تعالى ولو شاء ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلهم جمعيات﴾ [يونس، ٩٩/١٠]: إنه على الإكراه أن يمنعهم قسرًا كما جعلهم شيوخا وشبابًا، ولكن شاء أن يبتليهم، كقوله : ولو يشاء الله لا نتصر منهم ، وقد شاء ذلك بالنبي وأصحابه ، ولكن أراد به مشيئة القسر، إذ ليس معها حمد ولا أجر. { قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } وقد بينا ما يدل على وهمه. على أنه من كان ممن سبق إلى قوله : يقول الله تعالى : وإن منهم لفريقا يلوون ألسنتهم بالكتاب لتحسبوه من الكتاب وما هو من الكتاب ويقولون هو من عند الله وما هو من عند الله ويقولون على الله الكذب وهم يعلمون ﴾ [آل عمران، ۷۸/۳].
And He said regarding His saying Exalted: 'And if your Lord willed, all who are on the earth would have believed, all of them together' [10:99]: it is about compulsion, to prevent them by force just as He made them old and young, but He willed to test them, like His saying: 'And if God willed, He would have taken vengeance on them' [47:4], and He willed that with the Prophet and his companions, but He intended by it the will of compulsion, since there is neither praise nor reward with it. {The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said:} And we have shown what indicates his error. Moreover, he is among those who preceded to his saying: God Exalted says: 'And indeed among them is a party who twist their tongues with the Book so that you think it is from the Book, but it is not from the Book, and they say it is from God, but it is not from God, and they speak lies against God while they know' [3:78].
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation of Quran 10:99, which says that if God willed, all people would believe. The author (Maturidi) explains that this refers to a will of compulsion (mashī'at al-qasr), not a will of choice. God could force everyone to believe by preventing them from disbelieving, just as He determines physical characteristics like age. However, He instead wills to test them. The author supports this by citing Quran 47:4, which says that if God willed, He would have taken vengeance on them, indicating that He has the power but deliberately delays. The Prophet and his companions were subject to this same will of compulsion in some matters, but without resulting praise or reward because it is not a free choice. Then the author ('the Shaykh') states that he has already shown the error of the opposite view (likely that of Ka'bi, who misinterpreted the verse as indicating that God's will for belief is not effective). He then quotes Quran 3:78, which condemns those who distort scripture, implying that the ones who misinterpret are akin to those who lie about God knowingly.

Key Terms
مشيئة القسر (will of compulsion)
ابتلاء (trial)
إكراه (compulsion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'if God willed' implies that God's will is ineffective or that humans have no responsibility.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies it is a will of compulsion (not a will of guidance) that does not negate human choice; the test is genuine.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, the phrase 'if your Lord willed' in 10:99 refers to what type of will?
A) A will of guidance and reward
B) A will of compulsion without praise or reward
C) A will that is not actually effective
D) A will that depends on human choice

Key Takeaway
Maturidi distinguishes between God's will of compulsion, which forces actions without reward, and His will of trial, which preserves human choice and accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you relying on God to 'force' you into good deeds (like hoping He'll make you pray without effort) or are you embracing the trial of choice, knowing that only voluntary actions earn reward? Which area of your life are you avoiding personal responsibility by attributing your failures to 'God's will'?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you do it out of habit or pressure (like compulsion) or as a conscious choice to please God? If compulsion carries no reward, are you seeking reward by doing deeds only when they are easy or forced?
?
Maturidi says that even the Prophet was subject to compulsion in some matters but it brought no praise. In your relationship with God, are you content with a faith that is merely routine, or are you actively choosing obedience even when it is difficult, knowing that this choice is what makes it worthy of reward?

Refutation of Ka'bi's View on Divine Will
Maturidi refutes Ka'bi's misinterpretation and cites Quran 3:78.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of Ka'bi's misinterpretation of divine will and how he uses Quran 3:78 to warn against distorting scripture.

Core Idea
Misinterpreting divine will is akin to lying about God.

Having clarified the true meaning of Quran 10:99, Maturidi now turns to refute an opposing view, likely that of Ka'bi. He asserts that he has already demonstrated its error, and then quotes Quran 3:78, which condemns those who twist scripture to deceive others. This is a stern warning against theological distortion.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's claim of refutation
Start with his statement: 'We have shown what indicates his error.' This sets the stage for a conclusive refutation.
He refers to previous arguments not included here.
2
Identify the opponent and his predecessors
Maturidi says the opponent is among those who 'preceded to his saying' — meaning his view is not novel but follows a misguided tradition.
The opponent is likely Ka'bi, a Mu'tazilite.
3
Study the Quranic quote and its application
Read Quran 3:78 carefully. Notice how Maturidi uses it to equate the opponent's misinterpretation with knowingly lying about God.
The verse describes twisting tongues with the Book — a deliberate distortion.

Source Text

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi use Quran 3:78 to demonstrate?
A) That all people are liars
B) That distorting scripture is a known phenomenon
C) That Ka'bi's view is a lie against God
D) That the Quran is complete

Key Takeaway
Maturidi equates misinterpretation of divine will with knowingly distorting God's word, a serious error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran or listen to Islamic lectures, are you ever tempted to twist a meaning to fit your own desires or convenience? How does Maturidi's warning against distorting God's words expose a potential sin in your own heart?
?
What if your current understanding of Allah's will is actually wrong? Would you be willing to admit it and seek correction, or would you hold on to your view out of pride? This lesson says that stubborn error is like lying about Allah. How does that challenge you personally?
?
Have you ever defended a belief about Allah without checking if it is truly from the Quran and Sunnah? What concrete step will you take today to ensure your beliefs are not a distortion of the truth?

The Rational Refutation of Mu'tazila on God's Power
Rational argument followed by Quranic response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that God's power includes all actions, including human actions, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God cannot create human acts.

Core Idea
God's power is absolute and encompasses all actions, even human ones.

Maturidi begins by laying a rational foundation: God's power is not limited to what we perceive. He argues that those who think God cannot create human actions are actually denying His omnipotence. This passage presents the core logical refutation of the Mu'tazila position, culminating in the verse 'He is over all things competent.'

Guided Path
1
Read the rational argument carefully
Notice how Maturidi starts with a conditional statement about God's will and power. He argues that if God willed to create an act not attributable to creatures, He is fully capable.
The phrase 'until the Book comes with praise' implies that even the Quran affirms God's power over such creations.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim being refuted
The author says the Mu'tazila claim that 'God willed, but it was not' because He cannot create the actions of servants. This is the core error Maturidi addresses.
3
Understand the response via verse 5:120
Maturidi cites 'He is over all things competent' as a direct reply to the Mu'tazila limitation on God's power. This verse underscores God's absolute capability.
The verse is from Surah Al-Ma'idah, verse 120.
4
Reflect on the logical consequence
If God created the heavens and earth, why would He be unable to create human actions? Maturidi uses this analogy to highlight the inconsistency in the Mu'tazila position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الله لو شاء أن يخلق فعلاً ليس بفعل للخلق لا يقدر عليه حتى يجيئ الكتاب بالامتداح به والاقتدار عليه، وإنما قدر ذلك من الفعل في غيره مما ظهر من فعل آخر، وهو مما لا يبلغه حد البشر. فمن كان يظن أن الله يعجز عن هذا النوع من الخلق، فهو لا يقبل قدرة الله على حقيقة فعل الخلق؛ بل لو أريد ذا لكان موضعه فيما ظن المعتزلة أنهم خلقوا خلقاً وليس في العقول أرفع منه ولا أعلى في الحسن والفضل. فرمت هذا المعتزلة على ألسن الضعفة، فبين الله قدرته على مثل ذلك، وإلا لا وجه لإنكار مثله ممن يُقر له بخلق السموات والأرض وما بينهما. ولكن بين بذلك فساد قول المعتزلة: إن الله قد شاء فلم يكن، إذ هو لا يقدر على خلق أفعال العباد، فقال: وهو على كل شيء قدير ﴾ [المائدة، ۱۲۰/٥] جوابًا لذلك، وقال تعالى: فلو شاء لهداكم أجمعين ﴾ [الأنعام ، ١٤٩/٦] جوابًا للأول. وقوله: ﴿ولو يشاء الله لانتصر منهم ﴾ [محمد، ٤/٤٧] على أنه لو شاء تعذيب منذريه، بل لو شاء لانتصر منهم بما شاء، ولكن شاء التأخير. والثاني لانتصر منهم بهم، ولكن شاء أن يبلو صحابة نبيه بالهزيمة ليتبين الذين صدقوا كقوله تعالى: ﴿ ولقد فتنا الذين من قبلهم، وقوله: ومن الناس من يعبد الله على حرف الله وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: بيننا وبين القدرية الكلام في حرفين. أن نسألهم: هل علم
Indeed, if God willed to create an act that is not an act of creation, He is not incapable of it until the Book comes with praise of it and power over it. And He only measured that from action in other than Him from what appeared of another action, and it is something that does not reach the limit of humans. So whoever thinks that God is incapable of this kind of creation does not accept God's power over the reality of creating action; rather, if that were intended, its place would be in what the Mu'tazila thought that they created a creation, and there is nothing in intellects higher than it nor greater in beauty and virtue. This Mu'tazila cast on the tongues of the weak, so God showed His power over the like of that; otherwise there is no reason to deny such from one who acknowledges to Him the creation of the heavens and the earth and what is between them. But by that He showed the corruption of the Mu'tazila's saying: God willed and it was not, because He is not able to create the actions of servants. So He said: 'And He is over all things competent' [5:120] as a response to that. And He said Exalted: 'So if He willed, He would have guided you all' [6:149] as a response to the first. And His saying: 'And if God willed, He would have taken vengeance on them' [47:4] meaning that if He willed to punish His warners, rather if He willed He would have taken vengeance on them by whatever He willed, but He willed delay. And the second: He would have taken vengeance on them by them, but He willed to test the companions of His Prophet with defeat to make evident those who were truthful, as His saying Exalted: 'And We certainly tried those before them' [29:3], and His saying: 'And among the people are those who worship God on an edge' [22:11]. And Abu Hanifa, may God have mercy on him, said: Between us and the Qadariyya the discussion is about two matters: we ask them: Did He know... [Text cut off]
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that God cannot create the actions of human beings. The author argues that if God willed to create an action that is not attributable to creatures, He is fully capable. He criticizes those who think God is incapable of such creation, as this would deny God's absolute power. He notes that the Mu'tazila claim that humans create their own actions, and they consider that creation to be the highest in terms of goodness—but this is refuted by God's demonstration of power. God has made clear the corruption of the Mu'tazila assertion that 'God willed, but it was not,' because they claim He cannot create acts of servants. In response, God says 'He is over all things competent' (5:120). The author then cites several verses to show that God's will encompasses all possibilities, including taking vengeance or testing. He explains that verses like 'If He willed, He would have guided you all' (6:149) and 'If God willed, He would have taken vengeance on them' (47:4) demonstrate God's power and His choice to delay or test. The second part of 47:4 could mean He takes vengeance through the believers or tests them with defeat to distinguish the truthful. Finally, the author quotes Abu Hanifa, who says that the debate with the Qadariyya (a group related to Mu'tazila) revolves around two issues: asking them about God's knowledge—but the text is cut off.

Key Terms
قدرة الله (God's power)
أفعال العباد (actions of servants)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that since humans are responsible for their actions, God cannot create those actions.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's power is absolute and includes all actions, as shown by His creation of the heavens and earth and by the verse 'He is over all things competent.'

Checkpoint
Which verse does Maturidi cite as a response to the Mu'tazila claim that God cannot create human actions?
A) 'If your Lord willed, all would believe' (10:99)
B) 'And He is over all things competent' (5:120)
C) 'And We certainly tried those before them' (29:3)
D) 'And among the people are those who worship God on an edge' (22:11)

Key Takeaway
God's power is not limited; He is able to create all actions, including human actions, and the verse 'He is over all things competent' affirms this.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that God had the power to prevent it? How does that belief change the way you seek forgiveness and resolve to change?
?
If God is over all things competent, what does that mean for your reliance on Him in your daily affairs? Name one specific area of your life where you still act as if your actions are independent of His will.
?
The Mu'tazila claimed humans create their own actions. Have you ever acted as if your successes or failures are entirely your own doing? How would this verse (5:120) reshape your gratitude and humility today?

Quranic Evidence and the Qadariyya Debate
Quranic evidence and Abu Hanifa's statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses multiple Quranic verses to demonstrate God's power over human actions and His will, and how this connects to the debate with the Qadariyya.

Core Idea
Quranic verses prove that God's will and power encompass all events, including human actions.

After the rational argument, Maturidi strengthens his refutation with direct Quranic evidence. He cites verses showing that if God willed, He could guide all, take vengeance, or test believers—all of which involve human actions. This section also includes a quote from Abu Hanifa defining the terms of the debate with the Qadariyya, though it is unfortunately cut off.

Guided Path
1
Review the Quranic verses cited
Notice the pattern: each verse shows God's will is effective over human actions. Verses 6:149, 47:4, 29:3, and 22:11 are invoked.
2
Understand the interpretation of 47:4
Maturidi explains two possible meanings: vengeance by God directly or through believers, and the purpose of testing. This illustrates God's control over outcomes.
3
Reflect on the Abu Hanifa quote
Even though it is cut off, note that Abu Hanifa frames the debate with the Qadariyya around God's knowledge. This shows the historical context of the discussion.

God's Will Must Align with His Knowledge
Maturidi argues from the necessity of divine will matching knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that denying God wills things as He knows them implies ignorance and is blasphemous.

Core Idea
If God knows something, He must will it; otherwise, He would be ignorant.

Maturidi presents a razor-sharp argument to show that God's will cannot contradict His knowledge. Using a rhetorical question, he exposes the absurd consequence of denying preordination. Let's walk through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the back-and-forth questions.
The passage is a dialectical exchange between Maturidi and an imaginary opponent.
2
Identify the dilemma's first branch
The first question: 'Is it that God will never be as He is?' If the opponent says no, they disbelieve because they are ignorant of God. Identify why this answer is problematic.
3
Trace the second branch
If the opponent says yes (God is as He is), then a second question arises: Did He will His knowledge to be executed as He knew? If no, that implies God willed to be ignorant. If yes, all things are as He willed.
This shows that affirming God's existence forces consistency between His knowledge and will.
4
Understand the consequence
Denying that God wills things as they happen leads to the claim that God willed ignorance, which contradicts His wisdom. Therefore, the only rational position is that God wills everything exactly as He knows it.
The goal is to protect God's attributes of knowledge and wisdom from imperfection.
5
Reflect on the source
Maturidi attributes this reasoning to Abu Hanifa but notes it is not a verbatim quote. How does this attribution strengthen the argument?
It roots the argument in early Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله ما يكون أبداً على ما يكون؟ فإن قالوا لا كفروا لأنهم جهلوا ربهم. وإن قالوا نعم قيل شاء أن ينفذ علمه كما علم أو لا؟ فإن قالوا لا قالوا بأن الله شاء أن يكون جاهلاً ومن شاء ذلك فليس بحكيم. وإن قالوا نعم أقروا بأنه شاء أن يكون كل شيء كما علم أن يكون فهذا الذي تقرر عندي من المحكي عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لا أني ذكرته بلفظه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Is it that God will never be as He is? If they say no, they disbelieve, because they are ignorant of their Lord. If they say yes, it is said: Did He will that His knowledge be executed as He knew, or not? If they say no, they say that God willed to be ignorant, and whoever wills that is not wise. If they say yes, they affirm that He willed everything to be as He knew it would be. This is what is established with me from what is narrated from Abu Hanifa, may God have mercy on him, not that I mentioned it verbatim. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents a concise theological argument about the relationship between God's knowledge and His will. It begins with a rhetorical question: 'Is it that God will never be as He is?' meaning: is it conceivable that God's will contradicts His knowledge? The argument proceeds as a dilemma directed at an interlocutor. If they deny that God wills things as they occur, they commit blasphemy because they are ignorant of God. If they affirm that He wills, then they are asked: Did He will that His knowledge be executed as He knew, or not? If they say He did not will that, they imply that God willed to be ignorant—since He knew things would happen but willed otherwise—and one who wills ignorance for Himself is not wise. If they say He did will it, then they affirm He willed everything exactly as He knew it would be. Maturidi notes that this is what he has gathered from Abu Hanifa, though not a verbatim quotation. The argument is self-contained and shows the rational necessity of divine will matching divine knowledge to avoid attributing ignorance or futility to God.

Key Terms
علم (knowledge)
إرادة (will)
جاهل (ignorant)
حكيم (wise)
محكي (narrated)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will can be different from His knowledge; He may know something but will otherwise.
Correction: This passage shows that if God knows something and wills otherwise, He would be willing ignorance of Himself, which is impossible for a wise being.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if someone says God did not will things to be as He knew them?
A) He affirms God's wisdom.
B) He attributes ignorance to God.
C) He is still a believer.
D) He admits God's will is free.

Key Takeaway
God's will must always align with His knowledge; otherwise, He would be ignorant, which is impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every trial, every sin, and every moment of joy in your life is exactly as God willed it, based on His perfect knowledge? How does this belief change your response to hardship and your gratitude in ease? Commit to reciting 'رضيت بالله ربا' (I am pleased with Allah as Lord) today when something difficult happens.
?
If God's will must match His knowledge, then nothing in your life is random. Are you living as if you believe that? Where have you been attributing events to 'luck' or 'chance' instead of to God's will? This week, catch yourself when you think something is accidental and replace it with 'شاء الله' (Allah willed it).
?
The passage warns that denying God's will matching His knowledge leads to calling God ignorant. Have you ever, in your heart, doubted God's wisdom when things didn't go your way? Repent of that and ask Allah to increase your certainty that He knows and wills what is best for you.

The Contradiction of Commanding Sin
Maturidi presents the objection and the first reason: contradiction in command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why commanding a sin is contradictory while willing it is not, due to the transformative nature of command.

Core Idea
A command transforms an act; a will does not.

The objector asks why God's willing of sin is not blameworthy like His commanding it. Imam Maturidi begins his answer by highlighting a fundamental difference: a command can change the very nature of an act, turning a potential sin into obedience, but will does not have this effect. This lesson unpacks that first argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first reason
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the question and the initial answer.
2
Understand the contradiction
Notice how the command would nullify the sinfulness of the act, making it impossible to command a sin.
The key is that 'sin' becomes obedience by being commanded.
3
Distinguish from will
Contrast with will: an agent wills his own act but cannot command himself. This shows command and will are different.
This is a fundamental distinction in divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل إذ قبح الأمر بالمعاصي لم لا قبح إرادة كونها قيل لأوجه أحدها التناقض في الأمر وليس ذلك في الإرادة لأن الفعل ربما يصير طاعة بالأمر فمحال الأمر بالمعصية لأنه يصير بالأمر طاعة فيبطل معنى المعصية ليكون بها الأمر وليست الإرادة كذلك ألا يرى أن كل فاعل مريد لفعله ومحال أن يقال أمر نفسه بفعله ثبت أنهما مختلفان ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضاً إن الله يوصف بالإرادة في فعله ومحال أن يكون عليه أمر فيه فثبت أن أحد الوجهين ليس هو دليل الآخر مع ما كان الله تعالى مريدا هلاك الأنبياء والأخيار وبقاء الأعداء والأشرار والسعة لهؤلاء في الدنيا ولم يأمر بذلك بل أمرنا بالدعاء بهلاك هؤلاء وبقاء أولئك والله الموفق. وأيضاً إن فائدة الأمر رفعة الآمر وعلوه حيث استعبد الآخر وأظهر فيه حقه وعظيم مننه التي بها استحق أن يكون سيدا له ومعبودا وحق الإرادة الاختيار ونفي الغلبة أن لا يقهر ولا يمنع عن سلطانه ولا يحال بينه وبين ملكه وفي دفع الإرادة هذا لذلك بطل أن لا يريد وكذلك في المنع عن الأمر والنهي لذلك لزم القول بالأمر والنهي على الأمرين ليظهر سلطانه وربوبيته ولزم الإرادة في الكل ليحق ملكه وعجز الخلق عن أن يريدوا في ملكه وسلطانه والله الموفق. وأيضا إن الله أمر إبراهيم بالذبح والفداء بالكبش فلا يجوز أن يكون أراد فعل حقيقة الذبح ثم يمنع عنه بالبدل لأنه آية البداء وعلامة الجهل فكان الأمر لا بالذي به حقيقة الإرادة ولا قوة إلا بالله. وجملته ما بينا من انقسام معاني الإرادة والاتفاق على تحقيق المعنى الذي يذهب إليه وليس وراء ذلك إلا بمانع في اللفظ أو صرف عن جهته إلى جهة هي من تلك الجهة قبيح عند الخصم ولا قوة إلا بالله.
If someone says: Since commanding sins is reprehensible, why is willing their occurrence not reprehensible? It is said: For several aspects. One of them is the contradiction in the command, and that is not in the will, because the action might become obedience through the command, so it is impossible to command a sin, because through the command it becomes obedience, thus the meaning of sin is nullified, and the command would be for it; but the will is not like that. Do you not see that every agent wills his own action, and it is impossible to say he commands himself to do it? It is established that they are different. And there is no power except through God. Also, indeed God is described with will in His own act, and it is impossible that He should have a command over it. So it is established that one of the two aspects is not an indication of the other, especially since God Almighty wills the destruction of prophets and the righteous, and the survival of enemies and the wicked, and abundance for these in this world, and He did not command that, but rather He commanded us to pray for the destruction of these and the survival of those. And God is the Grantor of success. Also, indeed the benefit of the command is the elevation and highness of the commander, since he subjugates the other and manifests his right and great favors through which he deserves to be his master and worshipped. And the right of will is choice and the negation of compulsion, that he not be overpowered nor prevented from his authority, nor can there be any interference between him and his dominion. And in negating will from Him, this [negation] is [implied]; therefore it is false that He does not will. Likewise, in preventing command and prohibition, therefore it is necessary to affirm command and prohibition regarding both matters to manifest His authority and lordship, and to affirm will in all things to establish His dominion and the inability of creatures to will anything in His dominion and authority. And God is the Grantor of success. Also, indeed God commanded Ibrahim to sacrifice and then ransomed with a ram. It is not permissible that He willed the actual act of sacrifice then prevented it through a substitute, because that is a sign of reconsideration (bada') and a mark of ignorance. So the command was not accompanied by the reality of will. And there is no power except through God. The summary is what we have explained concerning the division of the meanings of will and agreement upon the verification of the meaning that he holds; there is nothing beyond that except a verbal obstacle or a turning from its proper direction to a direction that, from that direction, is considered bad by the opponent. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector raises a question: if commanding sins is bad, why is willing them not also bad? Maturidi gives several reasons (wujuh). The first reason is that there is a contradiction in commanding a sin because the commandment itself would transform the act into obedience, thereby nullifying its sinfulness; but will does not have this effect. An agent wills his own act but cannot command himself. The second reason: God is described as having will over His own acts but cannot have a command over them, so the two are different. Moreover, God willed the destruction of prophets and survival of enemies but did not command these; He commanded us to pray for the opposite. The third reason: the function of command is to show the commander's superiority and lordship, while the function of will is to show choice and absence of compulsion. Denying will would imply God is overpowered, so it is necessary to affirm will for all things. The fourth reason: the story of Ibrahim – God commanded him to sacrifice his son but then ransomed with a ram; if God had truly willed the actual sacrifice, He would not have prevented it, because that would imply reconsideration (bada'), which is ignorance. Thus the command was not accompanied by real will. The summary states that the meanings of will are divided, and the correct meaning is agreed upon; any objection is merely verbal or a misdirection.

Key Terms
أمر (command)
معصية (sin)
إرادة (will)
تناقض (contradiction)
طاعة (obedience)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first reason, why can't God command a sin?
A) Because sin is inherently evil.
B) Because the command would turn the act into obedience.
C) Because God does not will sin.
D) Because commanding is different from willing.

Key Takeaway
The first reason shows that commanding a sin is contradictory because the command itself would nullify the sinfulness, whereas willing does not have this effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever think, 'God willed it, so it's not my fault'? How does Maturidi's distinction between command and will challenge that excuse? What would change in your repentance if you truly believed God commanded you to obey but only willed the act?
?
If a command from God turns an act into obedience, does that change how you read the Quran? Are you treating His commands as mere information or as transformative directives that shape your very actions into worship?
?
You know God wills everything that happens, including your sins. Does that make you feel less accountable? Or does the fact that He did not command those sins strengthen your responsibility? How will you respond to the next sin you commit—will you blame God's will or accept your own disobedience?

Will in God's Own Acts vs Command
Maturidi gives the second reason: God's will vs command in His own acts and in history.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second reason: God is described with will in His own acts but not with command, and He wills things He does not command.

Core Idea
God wills but does not command His own acts.

The second reason further distinguishes will from command by noting that God is described with will concerning His own actions, yet it is inconceivable that He commands Himself. Moreover, God wills events like the destruction of prophets and survival of enemies, but He commands the opposite. This lesson explores this divine asymmetry.

Guided Path
1
Read the second reason
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the phrase 'God is described with will in His own act'.
2
Compare will and command in God
Notice that God has will over His own acts but no command over them. This shows they are different.
The distinction is not just theoretical but applies to God's attributes.
3
See the example of prophets and enemies
God wills the destruction of prophets and survival of enemies, but commands us to pray for the opposite. This proves that will and command can diverge.
The example illustrates the practical difference.
